In 2014, a video of the band went viral and helped set the stage for the group\u2019s meteoric rise.<br />\nIn 2016, the trio released their debut full-length album, Subway Gawdz, which featured guest spots from Kreayshawn, Armani White, members of Galactic, and Beats Antique. That same year they were featured on Beyonc\u00e9\u2019s Lemonade album, and performed with Beyonce and The Dixie Chicks at the CMA\u2019s.<br />\nOver the course of the next several years, Too Many Zooz continued to release EP\u2019s and singles - including ZombiEP (2019), a concept album about a fictional band playing a music festival during a zombie outbreak, plus collaborations and remixes with The Floozies, Beats Antique, Spencer Ludwig, Moon Hooch, Dot Cromwell, Nitty Scott, Lester London, and RoRo.<br />\nAll of that set the stage for the band\u2019s 2024 sophomore album Retail Therapy - a 13-song chronicle of auditory subway cinema that features appearances from Moon Hooch, Thumpasaurus, Lucky Chops, and Too Many T\u2019s."}

act = {"start": "2026-07-27 05:30", "end": "2026-07-27 07:00", "stage": "Grove", "act": "Frameworks", "url": "https://soundcloud.com/frameworks", "blurb": "\u201cI had to learn to get out of my own way,\u201d says Matthew James Brewer, the British songwriter, producer, and multi-instrumentalist better known as Frameworks. \u201cInstead of going out there chasing after songs, I had to learn to sit still and be present enough to let them come to me.\u201d<br />\nIn that sense, Reflections, Frameworks\u2019 entrancing new LP, is as much a work of cultivation as it is craftsmanship, a meditative exercise in tuning into the subconscious and capturing moments as they arrive. Recorded at home and primarily alone, the album is spare and hypnotic, eschewing the ornate orchestration of earlier Frameworks albums in favor of a lean, direct sound more akin to an internal monologue. The songs here mix driving electronic beats with layers of analog synthesizers and chopped up vocal samples, and the production is close-up and intimate to match, swirling around the kind of late-night thoughts that raise more questions than they answer. The result is a poignant contemplation of growth, purpose, and catharsis built on instinct and intuition, a bittersweet soundtrack for both connection and isolation, for discovery and loss, for the crowded, sweaty dance floor and the long, lonely walk home.<br />\n\u201cEven the more club-influenced tracks on this album have an element of vulnerability to them,\u201d explains Brewer. \u201cThey might be songs you could dance to, but they\u2019re also songs you could listen to on your headphones in bed well after the rest of the world has gone to sleep.\u201d<br />\nBrewer\u2019s been blurring the lines between dreams and reality for most of his career now. A Manchester native, he first rose to fame in 2015 with his critically acclaimed debut, Tides, which brought Frameworks\u2019 cinematic mix of instrumental hip-hop and downtempo, organic house music to an international audience. In the years that followed, Brewer would go on to release two more similarly well-received full length albums, perform everywhere from Coachella to Electric Forest, collaborate with the likes of Ninja Tune\u2019s Jono McCleery and fellow UK standout JP Cooper, and rack up millions of streams across platforms. When the pandemic hit, however, Brewer found himself forced off the road for the first time in years and unsure of what came next.<br />\n\u201cI was really inspired by architecture at the time, so I thought I\u2019d make an album called Forms that would be really focused on shape and structure and composition,\u201d he explains. \u201cI dove into it headfirst, but pretty quickly I realized that I was going about it all wrong. The more I forced it, the less natural it felt, and that\u2019s when it became clear to me that I had to stop trying to control everything and just let go.\u201d<br />\nAnd so Brewer slowed down and tuned in, treating his mind more like a receiver than a generator. He held his heart up to the light and examined what he saw: his hopes and desires, his doubts and fears. He reveled in the extra time at home with his family and in the beauty and strength they brought to his daily life.<br />\n\u201cMy wife and I have a son who was born with mitochondrial disease,\u201d explains Brewer. \u201cThey told us he wouldn\u2019t make it to his first birthday, but he\u2019s seven years old now, and though he may be non-verbal, I swear he\u2019s the happiest person I\u2019ve ever met. He\u2019s always present and always inspiring and every day with him is an absolute gift.\u201d<br />\nThe more Brewer surrendered himself to living in the moment, the more experimental and playful his music became. Songs began pouring out in spontaneous fits driven by emotion rather than intention, and soon a very different album than the one he\u2019d originally imagined started taking shape.<br />\n\u201cThe best things came very quickly,\u201d he explains. \u201cIf you press pause and try to come back to an idea later, it can be hard to get into the same state, so when a song would come to me, I\u2019d just try to lay as much of it down as I could all at once.\u201d<br />\nThat urgency forms the bedrock of Reflections, which opens with the relentlessly hopeful \u201cTwenty Two.\u201d Inspired by a half-marathon Brewer ran for his son, the track presses forward with a seemingly unstoppable momentum as manipulated vocals skip and dance their way across the surface of the music. Like much of the album, it\u2019s a deeply evocative track, one that manages to create an entire emotional world even without any explicit lyrical narrative. The pulsing \u201cBlue Light\u201d (one of three tracks featuring guest vocals from Australian singer Cleopold) conjures up visions of cruising the empty streets of a city late at night, while the invigorating \u201cCold\u201d captures the tension and liberation of emotional release, and the mesmerizing \u201cCircles\u201d finds peace and resolve on a journey deeper and deeper inside the self.<br />\n\u201cThe video for \u2018Circles\u2019 depicts a young woman dealing with her own internal struggle,\u201d Brewer explains. \u201cThe moments underwater reflect the battle within, and the final scenes show this moment of complete surrender and acceptance.\u201d<br />\nAcceptance is an integral notion on Reflections, and it comes up throughout the album. The insistent \u201cNo Time,\u201d for instance, circles around the same riff over and over like a mantra in effort to sit with (and welcome) the feelings that emerge; the hazy \u201cPorts\u201d draws inspiration from the strength and resilience of children\u2014not just his own\u2014that Brewer\u2019s seen battle difficult illness with unyielding optimism; and the reassuring \u201cSleep\u201d works through the stress and anxiety that often leaves us lying awake in the middle of the night.<br />\n\u201cI was having real problems falling asleep during the pandemic, and \u2018Sleep\u2019 was a direct response to that,\u201d explains Brewer. \u201cI wanted it to feel like a loving arm to wrap around someone struggling with insomnia, a comforting voice to let them know they\u2019re not alone.\u201d<br />\nAnd ultimately, that\u2019s the true magic of Brewer\u2019s music. Though these songs were created in isolation, they remind us of our shared humanity, of the unifying frequencies we can all tap into if we allow ourselves to slow down, be present, and embrace the moment. Still waters, after all, offer the truest reflections."}
